Preface
This MasterвҖҷs thesis is submitted after a two years study at Technical University of Denmark
(DTU) and as a partial fulп¬Ғllment for the degree, MasterвҖҷs of Science in Civil Engineering.
The thesis is a Master of Science project that corresponds to 30ETCS points and has been
carried out in the period from September 31th 2020 to January 31th 2021. The work has
been supervised by Assistant Professor Philip Skov Halding from DTU.

Abstract
The shape of arches has been used for centuries to build bridges, and have the last п¬Ғfty
years followed the development of materials, construction methods, and design theory. This
master thesis is an analysis project that investigates concrete sandwich arch bridges using
the pearl-chain (PC) technology developed by the Technical University of Denmark. The
main focus area of this report is to discover the worst position of the load cases through
calculations. Further, this information is used to п¬Ғnd which parameters that inп¬Ӯuence the
results.
A calculation method of the bending moment in the arch is shown and used as a basis for
the rest of the calculations during the project. The calculation shows that the static system,
shape, and geometry are all aп¬Җecting the bending moment. In addition, the diп¬Җerent traп¬ғc
load cases, load model 1 and load model 3, used in the investigation are presented.
Normal concrete arch bridges have been investigated to get an overview of the behavior of
the bridge before introducing the sandwich arch bridge. However, the critical positions of
the axle loads, as expected, occur in the area around the quarter-point, which is the worst
position of a single point load to be applied to an arch.
Sandwich arch bridges consist of prestressed concrete elements in both top plate and arch,
with a п¬Ғlling material between the two parts that transfers the vertical loads. The method
to analyze the sandwich arch bridges is described by using the sandwich eп¬Җect, where the
applied moment from the traп¬ғc load was taken as a force couple in the arch and the top
plate. It is the horizontal components in focus, and the total normal force in cross-sections is
composed of the normal force from applied load, post-tensioning, and self-weight. Further,
there is two way of looking at the sandwich arch bridge, as a beam or an arch. However, it
is not preferred to look at it as a beam because of high bending moments and no horizontal
components from the applied load, which results in a signiп¬Ғcant increase of the cross-sections.
The results from the analysis of sandwich arch bridges as an arch show that the critical
positions are more varying in the highest bridges. The thickness of the п¬Ғlling and static
system was the two parameters with the most inп¬Ӯuence, and the critical position is not the
same for the top plate and the arch. The self-weight of the п¬Ғlling material on circular shapes
with a high rise will be the dimensional factor because of the signiп¬Ғcant bending moment.
For an unevenly loaded bridge, in general, the highest total normal force was found to be in
the area where the eп¬ғcient height is thinnest and the capacity is small. It is on the loaded
side of the span for the top plate and on the unloaded side for the arch.

Introduction
The super-light structures technology with pearl-chain reinforcement is a new concrete tech-
nology invented at the Technical University of Denmark. The concept opens the possibilities
to make arches, vaults, cupolas, and curved shapes of any kind economically aп¬Җordable [1].
The super-light technology can be used in diп¬Җerent structures like buildings, bridges, and
tunnels. The technology research is still under development, and one of the interesting areas
is the arch bridges.
This is a master project that looks at the new arch concept with the sandwich eп¬Җect, which
is a part of the super-light technology development. Some projects about the concept have
already been developed, and the next part of the research is this thesis, which is about
investigating the concrete sandwich arch bridges.
1.1
Scope of Project
This thesis aims to analyze concrete sandwich arch bridges that are exposed to traп¬ғc loads.
It is desirable to understand how the forces of a sandwich arch bridge behave with applied
load and doing analytical work with the sandwich arch bridges to п¬Ғgure out how they work
statically. The project will focus on the sandwich arch bridgeвҖҷs behavior and the loadвҖҷs
most critical positions with associated cross-sections. The two arch shapes, parabolic and
circular, are presented and used in the investigation throughout the project. Both normal
concrete arch bridges and sandwich arch bridges have been investigated with diп¬Җerent traп¬ғc
loads and conditions. The project aims to hopefully get some results that can be useful in
further researches on sandwich arch bridges with concrete elements.
1.2
Outline of Thesis
As a guide for the reader, the content of the diп¬Җerent chapters is presented shortly in the
following.
вҖў Chapter 2 presents the theory of arches, including static system, shape, and geometry.
A basic analysis of arches is done to visualize the behavior of arches with three diп¬Җerent
loads. Also, the pearl-chain technology is introduced.
вҖў Chapter 3 concerns relevant loads, load cases, and load combinations, including bridge
1
==PAGE:18==
1.2. OUTLINE OF THESIS
classes with related loads, magnitudes, distances, and geometries. A small description
of how the static systemвҖҷs calculations have been done through the project is presented,
including a comparison of analytical and numerical calculations.
вҖў Chapter 4 is regarding an investigation of a normal concrete arch bridge with diп¬Җerent
load cases.
Traп¬ғc load has been placed adjacent to each other and in the most
undesirable way when applied to a given bridge structure. The buildup of the bridge
is described in п¬Ғve steps and has taken the construction process into account.
вҖў Chapter 5 is about the sandwich concrete arch bridge and introduce the sandwich
eп¬Җect. Two calculation methods are considered based on the sandwich structure; a
beam and an arch. The analytical work results are presented with the main focus on
the critical position of the traп¬ғc loads.
The progress of the thesis is illustrated in Figure 1.2.1 with the diп¬Җerent chapters.
Figure 1.2.1: Progress of the thesis

Theory
In this chapter, some history and development of arch bridges are presented. General theory
related to arch structures and shapes has been introduced, together with some calculations
of bending moment of diп¬Җerent arches. The theory and calculations act as a foundation for
further calculations. In addition, some of the bridge types and the technology used when
developing the analysis of concrete sandwich arch bridges are explained.
2.1
Background
Shapes of arches have been used since prehistoric times and were п¬Ғrst used by Mesopotamian
civilizations. They discovered the advantages of arch shapes in construction and started to
use corbel construction techniques.
In the beginning, the shape was used to build arch
entranceways and small arch bridges. By the time of the Romans, it was developed rules
for dimensioning the archвҖҷs shape and the wedge-shaped stones. At this time, most of the
bridges were constructed with stone or masonry and used as road bridges and aqueducts to
transport water. Some of the п¬Ғrst arch bridges built by the Roman are existing today [2].
The historic bridge Pont du Gard at NГ®mes in France (Figure 2.1.1) is still standing and is
composed of 52 arches divided into three tiers.
Figure 2.1.1: Pont du Gard at NГ®mes in France [3]
3
==PAGE:20==
2.1. BACKGROUND
The Middle Age was a period where European bridge-building received less attention and
slowed considerably until the Renaissance, when they started to build more bridges again.
The Renaissance also gave impulses for further development in bridge construction. The
Roman bridge-building technique was strongly bonded since the load-bearing arch should
be semicircular. It had been relatively small new development and construction of arch
bridges in Europe. From the thirteenth century, the bridge builders developed bold and
slim arch constructions with segment and elliptical shapes [2] [4].
In the eighteenth-century, iron was used in bridges and allowed for new possibilities and
daring construction.
Not long after iron was introduced, the exposure of concrete arch
bridges happened at the end of the nineteenth century. It was not until the idea of reinforced
concrete that it became useful in bridge construction [2]. Concrete is weak in tension and
strong in compression, which makes it suitable for arch bridges. Advantages with reinforced
concrete are that the reinforcement carries the tensile stresses, and the concrete carries the
compression. Additionally, the arch structure is good at transferring forces, and the arch
uses its shape to press the forces outward along the curve of the arch to the end supports
instead of downward.
Nineteenth-century was a period where more and more bridges get built, especially in Europe
compared to earlier years.
The construction builders started to build arch bridges with
longer spans. At this time, it was possible because of the new materials as iron, steel, and
concrete. The short and medium arch bridges were built with masonry, and compared with
long-span bridges, the masonry was not competitive against the new materials. Towards the
early twentieth century, steel and reinforced concrete decreased masonry archesвҖҷ popularity
because of the elastic properties in the latest materials [2].
Over the last п¬Ғfty years, arch bridges have followed the development of materials, construc-
tion methods, and design theory. Most of the arch bridges are used for roads, footpaths,
and railways. Some of the arch bridges built in this period are the Wanxian Yangtze River
Bridge in China (1997), Svinesundsbrua between Sweden and Norway (2005) and Gateshead
Millennium Bridge in England (2002) showed in Figure 2.1.2, respectively. An arch is a form
that has existed for centuries, and the construction techniques are still in development.
(a) Wanxian Yangtze River Bridge
(b) Svinesundsbrua
(c) Gateshead Millennium Bridge
Figure 2.1.2: Constructed arch bridges [5]
4
==PAGE:21==
2.2. ARCHES
2.2
Arches
An arch is usually deп¬Ғned as; a vertically curved and axially compressed structural member
spanning an opening and are supported on either side [2]. One of the essential features of
arched structures is that horizontal reactions occur even if the structure is subjected to
vertical loads only [6]. When the rise decreases, the outward thrust force increases. Since
the archвҖҷs rise is related to outward thrust, the internal ties or external bracing need to be
employed to prevent the arch from collapsing and maintaining the arch action if the thrust
force gets too high [6].
Arches develop horizontal reaction forces, which in turn reduce the design bending moment
compared to a simply supported beam without horizontal reaction forces. In the plot pre-
sented in Figure 2.2.1, an arch has been compared with a beam with the same span and
similar loading conditions. The two structures are loaded with a point load in quarter-point
and show the eп¬ғciency of an arch compared to a beam.
Figure 2.2.1: Comparison of an arch and a simply supported beam
The red curve shows the moment distribution for the arch, and the purple curve shows the
moment distribution for the simply supported beam. In the arch shape case, the bending
moment below the point load is calculated to less than half of the bending moment from
the simply supported beam.
The development of horizontal thrusts at the supports is one of the most important char-
acteristics of an arch as well as the vertical reactions, even if there are nonacting horizontal
loads. The moments and shear forces at any section of the arch are signiп¬Ғcantly reduced
because of the presence of the horizontal thrusts [7]. The bending moment is signiп¬Ғcantly
reduced with the use of an arch because of the presence of the horizontal thrusts. An arch
is more eп¬ғcient to use and a more economical design than other structures like the simply
supported beam [7].
ArchesвҖҷ behavior does not only depends on the horizontal forces but also their structure,
shape, height, and length.
5
==PAGE:22==
2.2. ARCHES
2.2.1
Arch structures
The classiп¬Ғcation of an arch is based on how it supports the lateral loads, and not its shape
and geometry [8]. Arches can be classiп¬Ғed as three-hinged arches, two-hinged arches, and
п¬Ғxed arches. The static system of the three arches is illustrated in Figure 2.2.2. A three-
hinged arch is a statically determinate structure with three hinges, one at each support, and
the third is placed at the crown (top point of the arch). A two-hinged arch is a statically
indeterminate structure to one degree of freedom. The degree of statically indeterminacy
is one because there are only three equilibrium equations available, and the arch has four
unknown reactions. The hinged are placed at the supports. The п¬Ғxed arch has non hinges
and is statically indeterminate structures to three degrees of freedom.
The arches are sensitivity to secondary eп¬Җects, such as arch shortening and foundation
settlement yielding of supports, and temperature variations [2]. The п¬Ғxed arch is sensitive
to relative settlement at the supports as well as any additional forces created by changes
in temperature [6]. In comparison, the two-hinged arch is somewhat insensitive to relative
settlement at the supports [6]. Since the three-hinged arch has three hinges, they are not
aп¬Җected by settlement or the additional forces created by temperature changes [6]. The
secondary eп¬Җects are decided by the degree of static indeterminacy, and they will decrease
with more hinges.
(a) Three-hinged
(b) Two-hinged
(c) Fixed
Figure 2.2.2: Static system of an arch [2]
The three diп¬Җerent arch systems presented here are the three most common, but three-hinged
arch and two-hinged arch are most commonly used in practice. Further in the project, only
the structural system of three-hinged and two-hinged arches will be investigated.
2.2.1.1
Shapes of arches
The most common arch shapes in the modern arch design are parabolic, circular, and cate-
nary. In this project, only parabolic and circular curvature of the arches will be used. The
shape of arches, together with the length and height, will aп¬Җect the forces that occur in the
arch.
Theoretically, a parabolic arch has a bending moment equal to zero at any cross-section of
the arch when the arch is subjected to uniform load. This is because the shape of the arch
and the shape of the bending moment has the same parabolic shape. This results in pure
compression for the arch [6]. This has been further investigated, and some calculations have
been done in Section 2.2.2 to see how the two diп¬Җerent arch shapes, parabolic and circular,
resist the loads.
6
==PAGE:23==
2.2. ARCHES

curve is needed. The equation for the height of a parabolic shape is shown in Equation 2.2.1.
The height of a circular curve is deп¬Ғned with an equation that depends on the archвҖҳs radius.
The formulas for the radius and the height of the circular shape is shown in Equation 2.2.2
and 2.2.3 respectively.

radius of the archвҖҷs curvature.
2.2.1.2
Ris-to-span ratio
The rise-to-span ratio is the relation between the rise (f) and the span (L) of the arch. It
is important for both the structural behavior and the appearance. The most common is to
have the rise-to-span ratio between 1:3-1:6. The rise-to-span ratio could also be between
1:2-1:10, but preferably not outside this range. These numbers are due to structural and
economic points of view [2] [9].
Horizontal thrust decrease along with the increase of the height of the arch. Signiп¬Ғcant
ratios are not in favor because of the small horizontal thrust, which gives a large bending
moment when the rise increases because of the self-weight of the arch and structure be-
haviors. Conversely, a relatively large horizontal compression force will occur if the arch
has a small rise-to-span ratio. The big horizontal thrust will provide high costs in terms of
foundation unless the rock foundation soil is rock [2].
The graph in Figure 2.2.3 shows the horizontal reaction forces when the height of the arch
is changing. As seen in the п¬Ғgure, low arches give signiп¬Ғcant horizontal forces while higher
arches reduce the horizontal forces.
Figure 2.2.3: Horizontal reaction force compared with height of an arch
7
==PAGE:24==
2.2. ARCHES
In practice, the construction method of an arch bridge is very important. A high arch that
needs many materials and big and complicated foundations is not preferable. When a bridge
has a high arch, ramps, or another way to enter the bridge is needed, it will make the bridge
more expensive and the construction method more complicated. Neither a very steep nor a
very п¬Ӯat arch is not very desirable considering the economy, appearance, and diп¬ғculties in
the execution [9]. So, the rise-to-span ratio is a key parameter when looking at arches.
2.2.2
Three-hinged and Two-hinged arches
The diп¬Җerent geometry and shapes of arches have been further investigated to see how the
arches resist the diп¬Җerent loads. The length has been constant when the height has been
changed for both two- and three-hinged arches. Three diп¬Җerent load cases are presented with
four diп¬Җerent heights. The diп¬Җerent loads applied to the arch are uniformly distributed load
(UDL), UDL on only half of the arch, and point load (PL). This is done for both parabolic
and circular shape. The red curve indicates the parabolic shape, and the blue curve shows
the circular shape. The span length is 30 meters in all cases, and the height varying between
3 and 15 meters. The heights are changing to see the results of its inп¬Ӯuence on the forces in
the arch. All the calculations for the three load cases with diп¬Җerent heights can be found in
Appendix A and Appendix B.
2.2.2.1
Uniformal distributed load (UDL)
The moment distribution of three- and two hinged parabolic and circular arches with applied
UDL are shown in Figure 2.2.4 - 2.2.7. It is clear that the moment distribution for both
the three-hinged and two-hinged parabolic arch is equal to zero. One of the principles of
using a parabolic arch is that the internal compression from the applied UDL will follow
a parabolic proп¬Ғle. So, when a parabolic arch is subjected to UDL, it is not subjected to
bending moment at any cross-section, and the arch supports the load in pure compression.
Since the two-hinged arch has only two hinges, one on each support, the bending moment
is not zero at the middle of the circular arch. In addition, using two hinged arches, the
moment is reduced compared with the use of three-hinged arches. For both three- and two-
hinged arches, the moment distribution gets bigger with the height of the arch.

2.3
Arch bridges
Arch bridges can be classiп¬Ғed depending on the location of the deck and the arch rib. The
three most common arch types are deck arch bridge, through arch bridge, and half-through
arch bridge, as shown in Figure 2.3.1.
(a) Deck arch bridge
(b) Through arch bridge
(c) Half-through arch bridge
Figure 2.3.1: Arch bridge types [2]
There are diп¬Җerent factors that aп¬Җect the choice of bridge type. Some of the factors that
need to be considered are the requirements for clearance under the bridge, the weight of the
traп¬ғc that should be carried, the length of access ramps, and the desired length and width
proп¬Ғle [9] [10]. The diп¬Җerence to use a deck arch bridge compared to the through arch bridge
and the half-through arch bridge is the freedom according to the design of the transverse
direction of the bridge. When using a through arch bridge or half-through arch bridge, the
arch structure need to be divided into two arch ribs and be placed with a distance, so it is
a place for a traп¬ғc area between, or the arch structure needs to be between diп¬Җerent traп¬ғc
areas [9].
2.3.1
Filled spandrel arch bridges
The deck arch bridge can be characterized as an open-spandrel arch bridge and a closed-
spandrel arch bridge [2]. The open-spandrel arch bridge has columns or walls resting on the
arch and supporting the road with п¬Ӯoor beams. The columns or walls transfer the loads onto
the arch, while the closed-spandrel arch bridge has a п¬Ғlling material at the spandrel where
the loads are transferred through and then onto the arch. The п¬Ғlling of closed-spandrel
13
==PAGE:30==
2.4. THE PEARL-CHAIN TECHNOLOGY
arch bridges is usually solid materials and only transfer vertical loads to the arch [11]. The
spandrel is the area between the arch and the bridge deck.
2.4
The Pearl-chain Technology
The pearl-chain reinforcement is a new invention made during the development of the super-
light concrete concept at DTU [12].
The technology is to post-tension precast concrete
units pulled on a steel wire through replaced cable ducts like a pearl-chain which create
a stable п¬Ғnal arch. Then the cables are tensioned, and the segments are in compression
[13]. Diп¬Җerent curves and shapes can be created by using the pearl-chain reinforcement with
simple prefabricated core elements assembled by prestressing wires or rods. There will not
be bending moments from the force from post-tensioning when the post-tensioning cables
follow the chosen arch shape and suited inside the curve ducts. Post-tensioning force also
introduces only normal stresses in the arch [11].
Pearl-chain consists of several prefabricated segments of material with suп¬ғcient strength
that are cast or extruded. It is designed as a chain where the segments are assembled by
one or more prestressing elements such as wires or lines. There are holes in each segment
where the prestressing elements pass through [1].
The joints between the prefabricated
concrete elements consist of mortar. Figure 2.4.1 shows examples of concrete cross-sections
of pearl-chain reinforcement with prestressing wires.
Figure 2.4.1: Examples of cross-sections of pearl-chain reinforcement [1]
The segments are prestressed when the wires are stressed and anchored at the ends. Because
the prestressing wire follows the same path as the chain and the elements are joined in
compression, the pearl-chain is unaп¬Җected by the path. Since the prestressing wire follows
the same path and combines segments with diп¬Җerent curvatures and straight elements, many
diп¬Җerent shapes can be made that have previously been not possible, which makes it more
п¬Ӯexible for architects [1]. Figure 2.4.2 shows a section of a prestressed curved PC segment.
14
==PAGE:31==
2.4. THE PEARL-CHAIN TECHNOLOGY
Figure 2.4.2: Prestressed curved pearl-chain segment [1]
A pearl-chain arch bridge is built up by several adjacent pearl-chain arches [14]. The width
of the bridge depends on the number of PC arches placed next to each other. After all the
pearl-chain arches are assembled, then the п¬Ғlling material п¬Ғlls out the shape of the structure,
and then a top plate can be placed above. A normal traп¬ғc way can be built on top of the
bridge.

Statics
In this chapter, the structural system which is used for further investigation in the project
is short described. Then, the loads are presented together with load cases and load combi-
nations. Also, a comparison of analytical and numerical calculations has been presented.
3.1
Structural system
The structural system is mentioned as the load-resistance subsystem of a structure. In this
project, it will be the arch of a closed-spandrel deck arch bridge. The structural system of
the bridge depends if it is a three- or two-hinged arch. The bridge works by transferring
the self-weight of the bridge and the variable loads through the п¬Ғlling material and onto the
arch. They are transferred as vertical loads. The forces that occur are related to the height
and width of the arch. The loads will be divided as vertical and horizontal thrust at the
abutments at the two sides of the arch.
3.2
Loads
The loads and load combinations that have been used are following the requirements in Eu-
rocode, including the additional national rules according to Danish Standard. The presented
loads are the self-weight of the structure and live load related to the design of a bridge.
3.2.1
Dead load
The self-weight that is used is the dead load of the three elements of the bridge; the arch,
п¬Ғlling material, and the top plate.
The dead load is calculated from the density of the
materials and the thickness. It has been assumed that the width of each element is 1 meter
throughout the whole project. The arch and top plate consist of prestressed concrete with a
density (ПҒc) of 2500 kg/m3. It is chosen a п¬Ғlling material for the pearl-chain bridge, which
is called pervious concrete with a density (ПҒpc) of 1900 kg/m3.
The dead load of the п¬Ғlling is calculated as many points load along the span of the bridge.
The area is divided into many small segments, so it is more distributed along the bridge
span than one concentrated point load.
The dead load of the п¬Ғlling was п¬Ғrst calculated as one point load in the center of gravity of
the sprandel on each side of the top point of the arch. The calculation methods used to п¬Ғnd
16
==PAGE:33==
3.2. LOADS
the point of gravity for both the circular and parabolic shape is found in Teknis StГҘbi [15].
These calculations gave a very concentrated load with a big bending moment. So, a more
precise distribution of the self-weight of the п¬Ғlling was done. The sprandel was divided into
smaller equal segments, where a point load that works in the middle of each segment along
the whole span corresponds to the self-weight of the п¬Ғlling. This calculation is optimized
and more like reality. The two diп¬Җerent calculations can be seen in Appendix C, and has
been done for both three- and two-hinged arches.
The result of the two diп¬Җerent methods to calculate the self-weight of the п¬Ғlling material is
shown in Figure 3.2.1 below. The red line indicates the bending moment with several equal
segments, while the green dot line represents the moment distribution from one point load
on each side of the crown. The bending moment is reduced a lot by calculating the п¬Ғlling
load more precise and realistic.

It is a small error between the area of the п¬Ғlling material in the two methods because the
segments are calculated each one at the time compared to the point load with the whole
area at once. The error can be ignored since it is a tiny percent of 0.08% that separates the
total area.
3.2.2
Live load
The live load that has been considered is traп¬ғc load, which is determined according to load
model 1 (LM1) and load model 3 (LM3) from Eurocode DS/EN 1991-2 [16]. These two
cases will give the most critical situations with unfavored positions of the loads.
For bridges exposed to heavy vehicles, bridge classes have been allocated according to load
requirements regarding Denmark. The bridge classes that have been considered are bridge
class 1 and bridge class 2, with a total pressure of 150 tonnes and 80 tonnes, respectively.
Each load model has been contemplated for both bridge classes. The calculations for the
traп¬ғc load can be seen in Appendix D.

3.2. LOADS
3.2.2.1
Load model 1
LM1 consists of two partial systems, a tandem system (TS) and a uniformly distributed line
load. TS contains point loads with diп¬Җerent magnitude and occurs in diп¬Җerent lines on the
bridge. Since the load case is looked at in a longitudinal section of the bridge, the worst
scenario with traп¬ғc load will be lane 1. When looking at lane 1, the TS consist of two axles
with a spacing of 1.2 meters. Simultaneously, as the TS, a uniformly distributed line load
is applied in the unfavorable side of the span.
The two point loads used in LM1 for bridge class 1 and bridge class 2 are shown in Equation
3.2.1 and 3.2.2, respectively.
P1 = ОұQ1 В· Qk1

P2 = ОұQ2 В· Qk1

found in the Danish national annex[17], and the point load, Qk1, is 300kN.
There are also two diп¬Җerent uniformly distributed loads for LM1 regarding the two bridge
classes, calculated in the two equations below.
Q1 = Оұq1 В· qk1
(3.2.3)
Q2 = Оұq2 В· qk1

Where the adjustment factors are Оұq1 = 0.667 and Оұq2 = 0.333, and the UDL is 9kN/m2.
For both the axle loads and uniformly distributed loads, the loads regarding bridge class 1
are the highest and most critical. They will be used in further calculations.
3.2.2.2
Load model 3
LM3 regards special vehicles with both axle loads and line loads. The two diп¬Җerent bridge
class situations with related axle load magnitudes and distance are shown in Figure 3.2.2.
Bridge class 1 of 150 tonnes has eleven axle loads and distributes over a span of 20.4 meters,
while bridge class 2 on 80 tonnes with eight axle loads distributes over a span of 16.2 meters.
In addition, a line load of 2.5 kN/m2 should be placed 10 meters away from the axle loads
and only at the remaining area of the unfavored part of the bridge.

3.3
Hand calculations
Most of the calculations are done with hand calculations. Three- and two-hinged arches
have been calculated in this project with two diп¬Җerent shapes, parabolic and circular. The
calculations of the forces in the bridges are based on the theory in Section 2.2.1 and 2.2.2
about static system and geometry. The loads applied are uniformly distributed loads and
point loads, which repeats with diп¬Җerent amounts and sizes through the project.
Analytical calculation of an arch with self-weight of the arch, п¬Ғlling, and the top plate has
been done for a three-hinged circular arch to show how the moment distribution is found.
The situation is shown in Figure 3.3.1, where RA and HA are the reaction forces, y(x) is the
height of the arch, q is uniformly distributed load and P n are the point loads.

3.4. FEM CALCULATIONS
Figure 3.3.2: Analytical bending moment distribution
The analytical hand calculation is compared with a numerical calculation from Autodesk
Robot. When using hand calculations, the curvature of the arch is considered as a п¬Ғne line.
The real condition of the curvature is more irregular and is more similar to the modeled
arch in Robot Structural Analysis.
3.4
FEM calculations
Autodesk Robot Structural Analysis is a structural load analysis and design software. It
is based on the п¬Ғnite element method (FEM). The п¬Ғnite element method is a numerical
technique used to perform п¬Ғnite element analysis (FEA) of any given physical phenomenon
[22]. Autodesk Robot can be used for diп¬Җerent structures and oп¬Җers a variety of ways to
presents the results. In this project, Autodesk Robot has been used as a tool to control the
hand calculations and get a better understanding and overview of the arches.
3.4.1
The arch
A 2D-model design frame is used to control an arch against the hand calculations. The
arch is modeled as a three-hinged circular arch with a height of 5 meters and a length of 30
meters with a pinned support in each end, as seen in Figure 3.4.1.
Figure 3.4.1: Model of the arch from Autodesk Robot
The arch is subjected to the same dead load as in the hand calculations, including self-
weight of the arch, point loads from п¬Ғlling, and a UDL from the top plate. The FEM model
considered both the self-weight and top plate on the real slope of the arch, while in the hand
22
==PAGE:39==
3.4. FEM CALCULATIONS
calculations, it has been considered as a line load on top of the arch. This will result in a
larger moment.
The numerical calculations of the bending moment can be seen in Figure 3.4.2, where the
bending moment is 380kNm. The numerical method gives a little bit higher moment than
the analytical. This is because of how the FEM - analysis calculates the UDL along the
length of the arch, while in the hand calculation, only the span between the two supports is
used. It is a simpliп¬Ғed way to calculate the uniformly distributed load.

Normal arch bridge
In this chapter, the two traп¬ғc load cases with load model 1 and load model 3 have been
investigated along the span of a normal concrete arch bridge. The traп¬ғc loads are placed in
diп¬Җerent ways in order to determine the most critical situation in terms of bending moment
and position of the loads for the arch bridge. Then the construction method of the arch
bridge is described and showed in п¬Ғve steps.
4.1
Normal arch bridge analysis
It has been investigated where the most critical position of the traп¬ғc load with load com-
bination 2 for both LM1 and LM3 are on a normal concrete arch bridge. It has only been
looked at bridge class 1 for both of the load cases because it is the bridge class exposed for
the heaviest axle loads. Diп¬Җerent heights, shapes, and static systems have been used for the
bridge. The width of the element that is considered is 1 meter.
The challenge for arch bridges is to consider heavy vehicle on only one half of the bridge
span. It will be the most unfavored placement of the loads since the point loads are non-
symmetrically.
Uniformly distributed traп¬ғc load is not a worst-case regarding bending
moment because of the shape of the arch. Some of the arches are designed to resist this type
of load [23].
4.1.1
LM1
First, the bridge is investigated with only п¬Ғlling up to the crown, and then 2 meters of п¬Ғlling
above the crown is added to see if the п¬Ғlling has any impact. Figure 4.1.1 shows the loads
and reaction forces of the bridge.
The worst position for an arch is a concentrated point load around the quarter-point. This
gives a big positive bending moment in the arch below the point and a corresponding negative
bending on the unloaded half side of the arch. In this case, the quarter-point is at 22.5 meters.
This can be used as an indication of where the positions are on the bridge according to the
quarter-point. The position which it refers to in the tables later, is the position of the п¬Ғrst
TS axle load. That means that the second TS axial load is placed 1.2 meters to the right
from the tables mentioned positions.

It can be seen from the results that for all the heights, shapes, and static systems, the most
critical position is between 23-24 meters. This is 0.5 meters away from the quarter-point,
and they are all in the same area. The extra п¬Ғlling will not change the most critical position
of the two axle loads from TS, but it will decrease the moment a little. This is because the
п¬Ғlling is a uniformly distributed load along the whole span and makes it more favorable for
the entire bridge, which results in a lower bending moment.
The most critical position of the two axle loads from TS is much more close to the mid-span
for circular three-hinged bridges with a height of 15 meters. This is because the positive
moment on the loaded side will never be bigger than the negative moment on the unloaded
side.

the bridge is changing. The positions of the loads, п¬Ғlling amount, and the static system is
the same. The bending moment for the parabolic shape increases with the height, while the
bending moment for the circular shape decrease with the height. This is in relation to the
fact that the moment distribution from the uniformly distributed load on parabolic arches
is equal to zero. In contrast, the moment distribution for circular arches gives negative
bending moments, as described in Section 2.2.2.

4.2
Construction method
Before the calculations and investigations of the sandwich arch bridges, it is important to see
how the concrete arch bridge is built up. The bridge consists of an arch with post-tensioning,
п¬Ғlling load up to the crown, extra п¬Ғlling above the crown (in some cases), and a top plate
to the end. The buildup of the bridge is illustrated in Figure 4.2.1.
Figure 4.2.1: The buildup of a normal arch bridge
The arch is built up by several concrete elements as a pearl-chain with mortar between the
elements. It needs to be considered which of the concrete or the mortar are the most critical
and which one should be used for the capacity check. It is assumed that the mortar is at
least 55 MPa [24]. So, the capacity would be determined with the strength of concrete of
55 MPa.
The arch has to resist the loads from the self-weight of all the diп¬Җerent parts. To see if each
part of the construction is satisп¬Ғed, the bending moment is calculated by the calculation
method shown in Section 3.3, where moment distribution for the self-weight with PL and
31
==PAGE:48==
4.2. CONSTRUCTION METHOD
UDL was determined. The moment capacity can be determined by means of the force couple
method according to Betongkontruksjoner [25].
The forces acting in a concrete cross-section of the arch is shown in Figure 4.2.2. It includes
normal force from self-weight of the arch and post-tensioning, tensile force, and compression
force.
The forces need to be in horizontal equilibrium, and the expression is shown in
Equation 4.2.1.
Fs = Fc вҲ’Ntot
(4.2.1)
Where Fs is the tensile force, Fc is the compression force, and Ntot is the total normal force
acting on the cross-section. The total normal force from the self-weight of the bridge is
found as contributions from the arch, the п¬Ғlling, and the top plate. The total normal force
in cross-section is the sum of all the horizontal reaction force contribution from the self-
weight and the post-tensioning. It is used the assumed distribution of compressive stress in
the compressive zone when determined the compression force, which is done with Equation
4.2.2.
Fc = (О· В· fcd) В· (О» В· xNA) В· b
(4.2.2)
Where О· is the strength reduction factor for high strength concrete and О» is the reduction
of the compressive zone. Both factors are dependent on the concrete compressive strength.
Figure 4.2.2: Forces acting in a concrete arch cross-section
To determined the moment capacity, it is needed to know where the steel is on the working
curve, if it has yielded or not (plastic or elastic).
This is done to п¬Ғnd the neutral axis
(height of the compressive zone). Then it is possible to determine the distance (z) between
the tensile force and compression force. Since the post-tension is positioned in the middle
of the cross-section, both the positive and negative moment capacity would have the same
magnitude. Equation 4.2.3 and 4.2.4 shows how to calculated positive and negative moment
capacity.
MRd.pos = Fc В· z
(4.2.3)
MRd.neg = вҲ’Fc В· z
(4.2.4)
It needs to be checked that the moment that occurs in the construction step is within the
moment capacity range that has been calculated. Since the moment capacity depends on
32
==PAGE:49==
4.2. CONSTRUCTION METHOD
the normal force from self-weight, the capacity will change during construction due that the
normal force changes every time a new construction part is applied. Therefore it is crucial
to do a check for all the steps when the bridge is built.
In the next sections, it is going through in steps how the bridge is built with the associated
bending moment and moment capacity. It is essential to check the diп¬Җerent steps in the
construction method so that there would not be some cases where the bridge would fail.
This is done for both a parabolic and circular three-hinged arch bridge. It is on the safe side
to look at the three-hinged since it has a little bit smaller capacity than the two-hinged. It
is important to be aware that the bending moment and moment capacity will change for
diп¬Җerent static systems, shapes, geometries, and cross-sections. In this project, there is only
looked at two cases (parabolic and circular) to get an understanding of the diп¬Җerent steps
during construction and how the arch bridge is built up. The whole calculations can be seen
in Appendix I.
4.2.1
Step 1: The arch
The п¬Ғrst step in the construction process is to put down the arch alone, which has to resist
the dead load of the arch and the post-tensioning. The arch needs to take all the dead load
from the bridge structure at the end, so the arch must have suп¬ғcient capacity from the
beginning. Figure 4.2.3 illustrate a pearl-chain arch with post-tensioning.
Figure 4.2.3: Step 1: Pearl-chain arch with post-tensioning
The capacity is determined according to the method described in Section 4.2 where it de-
pends on the forces acting in the cross-section. The normal force from post-tensioning is
calculated by Equation 4.2.5, where Ast is the total area of strand and fp0.7d is the pre-
tensioning level.
Npost = Ast В· fp0.7d
(4.2.5)
The bending moment distribution of the arch is calculated with a uniformly distributed
load. The normal force from the self-weight of the arch, together with the normal force from
post-tensioning, is the total normal force acting in the cross-section. Then the moment can
be compared with the capacity of the arch. As seen in Figure 4.2.4 , the arch has suп¬ғcient
capacity. The capacity of the arch is similar for the parabolic shape and the circular shape,
but the circular arch gives a higher bending moment.
33
==PAGE:50==
4.2. CONSTRUCTION METHOD

4.2.2
Step 2: Filling during construction
After the arch in the bridge is assembled, the п¬Ғlling material is the next step. This is the
step in the construction process that will be the most critical one. It is not possible to load
the arch with the entire п¬Ғlling material at one time due to the shape of the arch. The п¬Ғlling
material can be applied to the arch in diп¬Җerent ways. The easiest way for the contractor
and construction workers is to п¬Ғll half of the arch п¬Ғrst and then the other half. The moment
contribution, together with the moment capacity with п¬Ғlling load on only one half of the
arch, is shown in Figure 4.2.5.
(a) Parabolic shape
(b) Circular shape
Figure 4.2.5: Moment distribution and moment capacity of an arch with п¬Ғlling load on only
one side
It can be seen that the bending moment exceeds the positive capacity limit, and it has
to be done in another way to avoid increasing the cross section of the arch.
Since the
arch is divided into segments when calculating the bending moment from the п¬Ғlling load,
these segments can indicate how much to п¬Ғll at each time. The arch is divided into L/50
segments, which corresponds to 0.6 meters of the span. All the п¬Ғfty steps have been checked
and controlled that the capacity is satisп¬Ғed.
Figure 4.2.6 below illustrates the three п¬Ғrst п¬Ғlling steps where 0.6 meters of п¬Ғlling will be
34
==PAGE:51==
4.2. CONSTRUCTION METHOD
п¬Ғlled at a time. First on the left side, then on the right side, and then the left side again.
This will continue until the whole arch is п¬Ғlled with п¬Ғlling material. The associated bending
moment of the three п¬Ғrst п¬Ғlling steps for the bridge is shown in Figure 4.2.7.
(a) Filling step 1
(b) Filling step 2
(c) Filling step 3
Figure 4.2.6: Step 2: Filling during construction
(a) Filling step 1
(b) Filling step 2
(c) Filling step 3
Figure 4.2.7: Moment contribution during construction of the п¬Ғlling material of the bridge
When building up the п¬Ғlling material of the bridge, in this case, it needs to be done with not
more than 0.6 meters of the bridge at a time. It is important to emphasize that the bending
moment and moment capacity will change for diп¬Җerent static systems, shapes, geometries,
and cross-sections. A small guide to each project on how to build up the п¬Ғlling of the bridge
is necessary for the contractor and construction workers to build the bridge, so it has enough
capacity the whole time during construction and after.
4.2.3
Step 3: Filling
The third step is when all the п¬Ғlling is added, and it needs to be checked if the capacity is
good enough. Figure 4.2.8 shows the arch with applied п¬Ғlling material.
Figure 4.2.8: Step 3: Arch with applied п¬Ғlling
The procedure is the same as for the other steps, where the bending moment is compared
with the capacity. The moment from п¬Ғlling material is determined by using the п¬Ғfty point
35

4.2. CONSTRUCTION METHOD
loads from the segments that the arch is divided in and compared with the moment capacity
calculated by the total normal force from the arch, post-tensioning and the п¬Ғlling.
The moment distribution and moment capacity are shown in Figure 4.2.9. It can be seen
that the moment distribution for a parabolic arch is more signiп¬Ғcant than the moment
distribution for a circular arch, which makes the parabolic shape more critical when it is
compared with the moment capacity. The capacity is suп¬ғcient, and it is possible to continue
in the construction process to the next step.

Figure 4.2.10: Step 4: Arch with applied п¬Ғlling above the crown

4.2.5
Step 5: Top plate
The last step in the construction process of an arch bridge is the top plate, which is placed

Figure 4.2.12: Step 5: Arch bridge with п¬Ғlling and top plate
The top plate is calculated as a uniformly distributed load. So, for the parabolic shape,
it would not change anything on the moment distribution according to the fact that the
bending moment of parabolic shapes is equal to zero, but it will increase the capacity of
the bridge. For the circular shape, it can be seen that the moment decreases when applying
more uniformly distributed load, which is because UDL makes a negative bending moment.

Sandwich arch bridge
In this chapter, the concrete sandwich arch bridge is introduced by means of the sand-
wich eп¬Җect, and the calculation method for the axial capacity of sandwich arch bridges are
described. Then two diп¬Җerent ways of looking at the sandwich arch bridge have been in-
vestigated. Conclusively, the results of the sandwich arch bridges, which are subjected to
traп¬ғc load, are presented.
5.1
Sandwich eп¬Җect
The top plate of the bridge needs to be pre-stressed to become a sandwich structure. The
top plate consists of concrete elements laying next to each other with a duct in the middle,
and then the elements are post-tensioning together. This is done because the top plate
needs to resist some tension when it is used in a sandwich structure. The same pearl-chain
structure as the arch but only as a straight line, as seen in Figure 5.1.1, that shows the
buildup for a sandwich concrete arch bridge. It is assumed that all the self-weight of the
bridge is taken by the arch. Then when a load is applied, it п¬Ғnally works as a sandwich.
The sandwich eп¬Җect does not work before everything is put together.
Figure 5.1.1: The buildup of a sandwich arch bridge
The applied load on the bridge creates a bending moment, and these forces develop tension
and compression in the top plate and the arch. It has been assumed that the п¬Ғlling material
between the arch and top plate can transfer shear between the two elements. Therefore, it
39
==PAGE:56==
5.1. SANDWICH EFFECT
can be considered a sandwich bridge since the whole cross-section works as a homogeneous
structure [26].
Any applied load on the bridge will be calculated as resistant by the sandwich eп¬Җect. When
looking at the sandwich eп¬Җect, the horizontal components need to be considered, and the
moment from applied load can be taken as a force couple in the arch and the top plate. The
normal force Np(x) in the force couple is determined by Equation 5.1.1.
Np(x) = Mp(x)
H(x)
(5.1.1)
Where Mp(x) is the moment from applied load and H(x) is the eп¬ғcient height between the
force couple. The eп¬ғcient height, also called lever arm, can be found by Equation 5.1.2 and
it is assumed that the distance goes from the middle of the arch to the middle of the top
plate.
H(x) = (f вҲ’y(x)) + hfill + 1
2h + 1
2htop
(5.1.2)
Where f-y(x) determine the height of the п¬Ғlling between the arch and the crown, hfill is the
height of the п¬Ғlling above the crown, h is the height of the cross-section of the arch, and htop
is the height of the top plate. The eп¬ғcient height is changing in every cross-section along
the span.
When applying a point load in the quarter-point at the bridge, it will occur compression
and tension forces. The loaded side is in compression, and the unloaded side is in tension,
which is shown in the moment distribution in Figure 5.1.2.
Figure 5.1.2: Moment distribution from applied point load
The moment is redistributed as compression and tension in the bridge. It also indicates that
the normal force in the top plate on the loaded side will be in compression and the arch in
tension, and opposite on the unloaded side where the top plate is in tension and the arch
are in compression. Figure 5.1.3 shows the normal force distribution along the span with a
varying height from the applied point load.
40
==PAGE:57==
5.1. SANDWICH EFFECT
Figure 5.1.3: Normal force distribution from applied point load
When looking at the two sections in Figure 5.1.4, it can be seen which forces that act in the
two diп¬Җerent cross-sections. Section 1 shows the forces on the unloaded side of the bridge,
while section 2 shows the loads that occur in the cross-section right below the point load.
The normal force from dead load and post-tensioning acts in the arch, and post-tensioning
works in the top plate for both sections. In addition, the normal force from the applied
moment is applied as compression or tension in the top plate and the arch.
(a) Section 1
(b) Section 2
Figure 5.1.4: Normal forces acting in sandwich arch bridge cross section
The total normal force in the top plate and the arch can be calculated from equation 5.1.3
and 5.1.4, respectively.
Ntot.top(x) = Np(x) + Npost
(5.1.3)
Ntot.arch(x) = вҲ’Np(x) + Npost + Ndead
(5.1.4)
Since the horizontal components are important when looking at the sandwich eп¬Җect, the
normal force from post-tensioning in the arch needs to be decomposed into two components.
Figure 5.1.5 illustrates the internal forces in the arch. The normal force from post-tensioning
in the arch can be expressed by Equation 5.1.5 where Ast is the total area of strands and
fp0.7d is the level of prestressing force., and Оё is the angle between the slope of the arch and
the horizontal component.
Npost(x) = Ast В· fp0.7d В· cos(Оё(x))
(5.1.5)
41
==PAGE:58==
5.2. RESULTS
Strands used in post-tensioning of joined elements into a pearl-chain has a nominal cross-
section area of 150mm2, which is the most common type of strands in Denmark. The normal
force from the dead load (Ndead) is taken as the calculated horizontal reaction forces.
Figure 5.1.5: Internal forces in an arch
The capacity of the bridge is determined as the normal force capacity for the arch and for
the top plate because the moment from the applied load is converted into normal forces by
the force couple method. The following requirements in Equation 5.1.6 and 5.1.7 should be
fulп¬Ғlled for the top plate and the arch, respectively.
Ntot.top(x) < NRd.top = Atop В· fcd
(5.1.6)
Ntot.arch(x) < NRd.arch = Aarch В· fcd
(5.1.7)
The total normal forces have to be checked for the capacity in both the top plate and the
arch to resist the normal force as tension or compression. Ntot.top is the total normal force
that occurs in the top plate, while Ntot.arch is the total formal force in the arch. NRd is the
maximum compression capacity of the cross-section area (A) of the diп¬Җerent parts multiplied
with the strength of the concrete (fcd). In this project, it has been decided that the total
normal force in both the top plate and the arch should be above zero. That means that the
post-tensioning is more signiп¬Ғcant than the largest tension from the applied load, and then
all problems with cracks can be avoided.
5.2
Results
It is two methods to look at the concrete sandwich arch bridge when the normal force
distributions should be calculated; as a beam and as an arch. The diп¬Җerence is that the
beam will give a larger moment, but no horizontal reaction forces and an arch will give
smaller moments and horizontal reaction forces. Both methods are used to п¬Ғnd the most
critical position of the traп¬ғc loads on sandwich arch bridges and the associated cross section.
The axle loads from the load cases should be placed in diп¬Җerent ways to determine the most
critical situation.
5.2.1
Method 1: Beam
It would be non horizontal reaction forces from the applied load when the sandwich arch
bridge is looked at as a beam. This will give huge positive bending moments below the loads
and result in really high compression forces in the top plate and tension forces in the arch.
42
==PAGE:59==
5.2. RESULTS
Figure 5.2.1 shows an example of a bending moment and normal force distribution for a case
with TS loads positioned at the middle of the bridge.
(a) Moment distribution
(b) Normal force distribution
Figure 5.2.1: Moment distribution and normal force distribution from applied load
The big positive moment makes the whole top plate in compression and the arch in tension,
based on the sandwich eп¬Җect. As seen in Figure 5.2.2 it creates a total normal force in the
top plate that exceeds the maximum compression capacity in the cross-section of the top
plate. On the other side, the total normal force in the arch is in tension, which will lead to
cracks in the bridge.
(a) Top plate
(b) Arch
Figure 5.2.2: Total normal force in sandwich arch bridge together with the capacity
Further, it is only traп¬ғc load from LM1 that has been considered in this method. This is
because of the high forces that occur in the bridge, as mention above. The cross-sections
needed to be increased a lot to be satisп¬Ғed when looking at the sandwich arch bridge with
non extra п¬Ғlling. If we put some п¬Ғlling on top over the crown, the capacity for both top and
arch is satisп¬Ғed, but then for the highest bridges, the moment from dead load will be the
critical point, and the cross section of the arch needed to be increased. For the bridges with
a height of 3 meters and 5 meters, it is possible to calculate them like a beam if we have
at least 1.5 meters п¬Ғlling above the crown. To calculate the sandwich arch bridge with the
method as a beam, it depends on the height and geometry of the bridge.
43
==PAGE:60==

the bridge regarding normal force by moving the two axle loads from TS along the span.

position of the п¬Ғrst axle load and the gray boxes indicates the associated cross section. The

The critical positions of the loads will be diп¬Җerent for the arch and the top plate. The
most critical position of TS for the top plate is 15m for the п¬Ғrst axle load and 16.2m for
the second axle load. The most signiп¬Ғcant normal force occurs in the cross section at 15.1
meters, which is right next to the TS load position. The most critical position of the arch is
when the loads are placed at 28.8 meters and at the end of the bridge at 30.0 meters. The
most critical cross section occurs on the unloaded side.
Since the method by looking at the sandwich arch bridge as a beam could only be used on
a few of the bridges, the method is not preferred. It would create too big cross-sections for
both the arch and the top plate because of the big moment from the applied load. It shows
that the horizontal forces signiп¬Ғcantly impact the structure, and the structure should not
be assumed to act as a beam.
5.2.2
Method 2: Arch
The horizontal forces from the applied traп¬ғc loads are included when looking at the sandwich
arch bridge as an arch. This will reduce the moment considerably compared with method 1.
44
==PAGE:61==
5.2. RESULTS
The consequence is that the horizontal reactions have to be connected with studs somehow
in the foundations, but the impact the horizontal forces have on the bridge is favorable.
The applied traп¬ғc loads on the sandwich arch bridge will create a normal force by the
sandwich eп¬Җect. The bending moment and normal force will change depending on which
cross section it is looked at and where the load is positioned. The moment capacity depends
on the normal force, so each time the load is changing position, the normal force change,
and it also changes how much the bridge can resist. Therefore, every cross-section along the
whole bridge needs to be checked every time the positions change to п¬Ғnd the worst case.
Load combination 2 with traп¬ғc loads from LM1 and LM3 with bridge class 1 has been
investigated along the bridge span separately to see how the sandwich arch bridge is aп¬Җected
by the loads.
The sandwich eп¬Җect described in Section 5.1 is used, and the results are
presented in the two following sections.
5.2.2.1
LM1
The load situation for a sandwich arch bridge with LM1 is the same as shown in Figure 4.1.1
where it is the self-wight of the bridge and the loads from TS with two axle point loads and
uniformly distributed load on only one half side of the bridge.
An example of the calculated moment from the applied load is shown in Figure 5.2.3 which
creates both positive and negative moments along the span. The bending moment is de-
creased a lot by adding the horizontal forces compare with the bending moment from applied
load in method 1. The normal force that occurs from the applied load is also shown in Figure
5.2.3. This is determined by the relation between the bending moment and the height of
the bridge along the span. When the normal force reaches a positive value, the bending
moment is positive, which indicates that there is compression in the top plate and tension
in the arch on the loaded side. It is the opposite where the moment and the normal force
are negative. Then it will be compression in the arch and tension in the top plate on the
unloaded side.
(a) Moment distribution
(b) Normal force distribution
Figure 5.2.3: Moment distribution and normal force distribution from applied load - LM1
Further, the total normal force, including normal force from the dead load, post-tensioning,
and applied load, is determined for the top plate and arch and shown in Figure 5.2.4 together
45
==PAGE:62==
5.2. RESULTS
with the bearing capacity. The normal forces are below the maximum compression in the
cross-sections, and above zero, so the capacity is satisп¬Ғed for both top plate and arch.
(a) Top plate
(b) Arch
Figure 5.2.4: Total normal force and bearing capacity - LM1

The interesting part of the tables is to see where the position for the two axle loads gives
the highest normal forces. The two hinged bridges give the highest value of the normal force
with the position of the axle loads around the middle of the bridge. The results from the
tables also show that the position of the axle loads in the top plate for three hinged bridges
is at the same place, and the two hinged bridges are in the same place. It is similar for the
arch.
By adding the extra п¬Ғlling above the arch, the total normal force will decrease for all cases.
This is because the distance between the force couple from the applied traп¬ғc load gets more
signiп¬Ғcant, which results in lower normal forces from the traп¬ғc load based on the relation
between the bending moment and the eп¬ғcient height.
The bottom panes in the tables with a height of 15 meters are empty. This is because the
bending moment from the self-weight exceeds the moment capacity in the arch. The cross
section needs to be increased a lot in order for the capacity to be satisп¬Ғed. It is not wanted
to have huge cross sections either in the top plate or the arch. The large weight of the п¬Ғlling
in the sides will be the dimensioning factor for the arch bridge.
The bar chart shown in Figure 5.2.5 illustrates an overview of the position for the diп¬Җerent
static systems and shapes with varying heights. The height has not that big an impact on
the most critical position of the axle loads. For the top plate, it is more varying in position
compared with the arch. The three-hinged system for the top plate allows the axle load to
be placed a little closer to the quarter-point before the normal forces reach the maximum
value. It is opposite regarding the arch, where it is the two-hinged system that allows it.
48
==PAGE:65==
5.2. RESULTS
(a) Top plate
(b) Arch
Figure 5.2.5: Bar chart for the positions from diп¬Җerent heights - LM1
In addition, a bar chart for the arches with 2 meters extra п¬Ғlling is shown in Figure 5.2.6.
The critical positions varying in the top plate from height to height, and the positions move
closer to the end of the bridge with extra п¬Ғlling. The positions of the arch with the extra
п¬Ғlling are more similar to the position without п¬Ғlling and occurs around the same area of
the span. The bridges with a height of 15 meters with extra п¬Ғlling do not create a situation
in the chart based on the outcome from the table described above.
(a) Top plate
(b) Arch
Figure 5.2.6: Bar chart for the positions from diп¬Җerent heights with extra п¬Ғlling - LM1
When the bridge behaves as a sandwich structure, the critical cross section where the highest
normal force occurs is not right below where the applied axle loads act. The cross-section
exposed to the high normal forces is in diп¬Җerent places, but they are all in the same area for
the positions of the loads and the diп¬Җerent heights, shapes, and static system. As illustrated
in Figure 5.2.7, the critical cross-sections for the arch occurs on the unloaded side close to
the middle of the bridge distributed over an area of 4 meters. It is where the normal force
has the highest compression force due to the fact that the arch is in compression on the
unloaded side. The top plate has a bigger area distributed over 7 meters of the span and
is close to the quarter-point in the loaded side. For both cases, the critical cross-sections
occur where the bridge is thinnest and will have a smaller capacity.

moves out of the bridge span when the position is changing along with the span.

moment distribution and normal force distribution are shown in Figure 5.2.8 with both
positive and negative values. It is a case where all the eighth rear axles are applied, and it
turnoutsout to be the worst position of the loads for the arch in the bridges. The calculations
of the example can be found in Appendix N and the results from all cases can be seen in
Appendix O.

5.2. RESULTS
It is clear from the tables that the most critical position of the load regarding the arch is
in the middle of the span at 15 meters. Every shape, height, and the structural system has
the same critical position. The associated most critical cross section is in the area of 9-13
meters, which is on the unloaded side of the cross section. The critical position of the loads
regarding the top plate is in the area between 17-19 meters, where the LM3 consists of seven
axle loads distributed over an area of 8.4 meters. This makes the loads be balanced around
the quarter-point, which is the worst position for an arch to have applied loads.
The results from LM3 are very like the results from LM1, but the critical positions are even
more similar. The axle loads from LM3 are heavier in total than the two axle loads from
LM1, which creates more signiп¬Ғcant bending moments and results in larger normal forces
due to the sandwich eп¬Җect.
Figure 5.2.10 and 5.2.11 illustrates the critical positions for the arch bridge with diп¬Җerent
heights and with extra п¬Ғlling, respectively. The positions in the arch in both situations have
the same position. The positions for the top plate is around 1-2 meter more to the right on
the bridge with extra п¬Ғlling on top compared with the п¬Ғlling only to the crown.

(b) Arch

6
|
Conclusion
This projectвҖҷs ambition is set out to analyze and investigate sandwich arch bridges exposed
to traп¬ғc loads. It also intends to п¬Ғnd the loads most critical positions and п¬Ғgure out how
the bridges work statically.
The arch bridge is build up by pearl-chain reinforcement with concrete elements, п¬Ғlling
material, and top plate. The traп¬ғc loads were placed in diп¬Җerent positions along the span
and were investigated for the traп¬ғc load cases LM1 and LM3, both in bridge class 1. A
set of diп¬Җerent combinations of heights, shapes, and static systems have been created to
look at various aspects. The heights have been changed between 3, 5, 10, and 15 meters for
parabolic and circular three- and two-hinged arches. All the results are mainly focused on
the critical position of the axle loads from the load combination.
Before the sandwich method was introduced, the normal arch bridge was investigated with
traп¬ғc loads and diп¬Җerent bridge conditions.
The critical position of the axle loads was
around the quarter-point, which is related due to the fact that the worst scenario of an arch
is a concentrated point load around the quarter-point. The height of the bridge or the static
system did not have a signiп¬Ғcant impact on the positions.
For a sandwich arch bridge, the top plate elements and the arch elements need to be post-
tensioned before it works as a sandwich. Based on the sandwich eп¬Җect, a calculation method
for the sandwich arch bridge was developed to п¬Ғnd the horizontal components that needed
to be considered in the bridge. The applied moment from the traп¬ғc load was taken as a
force couple in the arch and the top plate. The total normal forces acting in the cross-section
were combined by the normal force from applied load, post-tensioning, and self-weight.
By using the sandwich eп¬Җect, the analytical analysis of sandwich arch bridges has been done.
The investigation has taken two methods into account, which has shown that the horizontal
thrust has a considerable impact on the sandwich structure. It is not preferred to look at the
bridge as a beam, because it needs to create large cross-sections for both the arch and the top
plate for the bridge to be satisп¬Ғed, and it is not possible to use the method without an extra
п¬Ғlling of 1.5 meters above the crown. It is recommended to look at the bridge as an arch
where the horizontal forces are taken into the calculations. The critical position diп¬Җerence
is higher by changing structural parameters as three- and two-hinged arches rather than the
heights. Circular shapes with a high rise-to-span ratio give too high bending moments from
the self-weight compared with the capacity. The large self-weight of the п¬Ғlling material in
the sides will be the dimensional factor for arch bridges with a high rise.
55
==PAGE:72==
6.1. FURTHER WORK
It is also interesting to see where the associated cross-section for the critical position oc-
curred. For both load cases, the essential cross-section for the arch is on the unloaded side,
while for the top plate, it is between the midpoint of the span and quarter-point on the
loaded side. They are all in the areas close to the mid-span of the bridge, where the eп¬ғcient
height is small and has less capacity than at the ends of the bridge.
The results found from the analysis about concrete sandwich arch bridges can be used as a
foundation in further research projects about the arch concepts with sandwich eп¬Җect.
6.1
Further work
Some suggestions for future work are presented in the following.
вҖў Based on the results that the self-weight of the п¬Ғlling material will be a dimensional
factor for some bridges, the next step could be to look at how the weight of the п¬Ғlling
in the sides can be reduced.
вҖў The horizontal forces from the applied load are included when looking at the bridge as
an arch. Further, it can consider how the horizontal reactions should be handled, how
the horizontal reactions aп¬Җect the foundation, and how they need to be connected in
the foundation.
вҖў In this project, the cross-section capacity was limit to above zero and below maximum
compression. A further investigation of the tension and possible cracks in the arch
could be done.

Figure 4.2b - Application of tandem systems for local verifications
Tandem system consist of two axels with a spacing of 1.2 meters, as shown in Figure 4.2a. At
the same time as the TS system, a UDL system is applied in the unfavorable parts of span (half
span, same side as TS). 
Lane 1 is the most critical lane, as heavy traffic load typically are considered in this lane. The
largest UDL and TS occurs at this line.

:=
Limited to two load cases which is the most critical ones. Most important loads are Load model
1 (LM1) and load model 3 (LM3), TS-system and spescial vehicles. Load model 4 (LM4) is a
uniformaly distributed load equal to 5kN/m2. Because of the shapes on the arch, is this load
model not that critical. It is the same for the single axle load in load model 2 (LM2), which is
less critical than the TS-system.
